  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/844be31dc6f060428bcd89ef00a62bf8c306c0b9" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:42.094ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle Z=\lambda f.(\lambda x.f(\lambda v.xxv))\ (\lambda x.f(\lambda v.xxv))\ .}"></span></dd></dl>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Standard_terms">Standard terms</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lambda_calculus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Standard terms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Certain terms have commonly accepted names:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="anchor" id="I"></span> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>I</b>&#160;:= λ<i>x</i>.<i>x</i></span></dd>
<dd><span class="anchor" id="S"></span> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>S</b>&#160;:= λ<i>x</i>.λ<i>y</i>.λ<i>z</i>.<i>x</i> <i>z</i> (<i>y</i> <i>z</i>)</span></dd>
<dd><span class="anchor" id="K"></span> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>K</b>&#160;:= λ<i>x</i>.λ<i>y</i>.<i>x</i></span></dd>
<dd><span class="anchor" id="B"></span> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>B</b>&#160;:= λ<i>x</i>.λ<i>y</i>.λ<i>z</i>.<i>x</i> (<i>y</i> <i>z</i>)</span></dd>
<dd><span class="anchor" id="C"></span> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>C</b>&#160;:= λ<i>x</i>.λ<i>y</i>.λ<i>z</i>.<i>x</i> <i>z</i> <i>y</i></span></dd>
<dd><span class="anchor" id="W"></span> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>W</b>&#160;:= λ<i>x</i>.λ<i>y</i>.<i>x</i> <i>y</i> <i>y</i></span></dd>
<dd><span class="anchor" id="omega"></span> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>ω</b> or <b>Δ</b> or <b>U</b>&#160;:= λ<i>x</i>.<i>x</i> <i>x</i></span></dd>
<dd><span class="anchor" id="Omega"></span> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>Ω</b>&#160;:= <b>ω ω</b></span></dd></dl>
<p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>I</b></span> is the identity function. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>SK</b></span> and <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>BCKW</b></span> form complete <a href="/wiki/Combinator_calculus" class="mw-redirect" title="Combinator calculus">combinator calculus</a> systems that can express any lambda term - see 
<a href="#Abstraction_elimination">the next section</a>. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>Ω</b></span> is <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>UU</b></span>, the smallest term that has no normal form. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>YI</b></span> is another such term.
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>Y</b></span> is standard and defined <a href="#Y">above</a>, and can also be defined as <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>Y</b>=<b>BU(CBU)</b></span>, so that <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>Y</b>g=g(<b>Y</b>g)</span>. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">TRUE</span> and <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">FALSE</span> defined <a href="#Logic_and_predicates">above</a> are commonly abbreviated as <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>T</b></span> and <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><b>F</b></span>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Abstraction_elimination">Abstraction elimination</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lambda_calculus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Abstraction elimination"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Combinatory_logic#Completeness_of_the_S-K_basis" title="Combinatory logic">Combinatory logic §&#160;Completeness of the S-K basis</a></div>
<p>If <i>N</i> is a lambda-term without abstraction, but possibly containing named constants (<a href="/wiki/Combinatory_logic" title="Combinatory logic">combinators</a>), then there exists a lambda-term <i>T</i>(<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>x</i></span>,<i>N</i>) which is equivalent to <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">λ<i>x</i>.</span><i>N</i> but lacks abstraction (except as part of the named constants, if these are considered non-atomic). This can also be viewed as anonymising variables, as <i>T</i>(<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>x</i></span>,<i>N</i>) removes all occurrences of <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>x</i></span> from <i>N</i>, while still allowing argument values to be substituted into the positions where <i>N</i> contains an <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>x</i></span>. The conversion function <i>T</i> can be defined by:
</p>
<dl><dd><i>T</i>(<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>x</i></span>, <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>x</i></span>)&#160;:= <b>I</b></dd>
<dd><i>T</i>(<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>x</i></span>, <i>N</i>)&#160;:= <b>K</b> <i>N</i> if <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>x</i></span> is not free in <i>N</i>.</dd>
<dd><i>T</i>(<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>x</i></span>, <i>M</i> <i>N</i>)&#160;:= <b>S</b> <i>T</i>(<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>x</i></span>, <i>M</i>) <i>T</i>(<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>x</i></span>, <i>N</i>)</dd></dl>
<p>In either case, a term of the form <i>T</i>(<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>x</i></span>,<i>N</i>) <i>P</i> is reduced by having the initial combinator <b>I</b>, <b>K</b>, or <b>S</b> grab the argument <i>P</i>, just like β-reduction of <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">(λ<i>x</i>.</span><i>N</i><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">)</span> <i>P</i> would do. <b>I</b> returns that argument. <b>K</b> <i>N</i> throws the argument away, just like <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">(λ<i>x</i>.</span><i>N</i><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">)</span> does when <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>x</i></span> has no free occurrence in <i>N</i>. <b>S</b> passes the argument on to both subterms of the application, and then applies the result of the first to the result of the second, just like <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">(λ<i>x</i>.</span><i>MN</i><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">)<i>P</i></span> is the same as <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">((λ<i>x</i>.</span><i>M</i><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">)<i>P</i>)</span> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">((λ<i>x</i>.</span><i>N</i><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">)<i>P</i>)</span>.
</p><p>The combinators <b>B</b> and <b>C</b> are similar to <b>S</b>, but pass the argument on to only one subterm of an application (<b>B</b> to the "argument" subterm and <b>C</b> to the "function" subterm), thus saving a subsequent <b>K</b> if there is no occurrence of <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>x</i></span> in one subterm. In comparison to <b>B</b> and <b>C</b>, the <b>S</b> combinator actually conflates two functionalities: rearranging arguments, and duplicating an argument so that it may be used in two places. The <b>W</b> combinator does only the latter, yielding the <a href="/wiki/B,_C,_K,_W_system" title="B, C, K, W system">B, C, K, W system</a> as an alternative to <a href="/wiki/SKI_combinator_calculus" title="SKI combinator calculus">SKI combinator calculus</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Typed_lambda_calculus">Typed lambda calculus</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lambda_calculus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Typed lambda calculus"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Typed_lambda_calculus" title="Typed lambda calculus">Typed lambda calculus</a></div>
<p>A <i>typed lambda calculus</i> is a typed <a href="/wiki/Formalism_(mathematics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Formalism (mathematics)">formalism</a> that uses the lambda-symbol (<span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle \lambda }">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>&#x03BB;<!-- λ --></mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \lambda }</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b43d0ea3c9c025af1be9128e62a18fa74bedda2a" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.355ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle \lambda }"></span>) to denote anonymous function abstraction. In this context, types are usually objects of a syntactic nature that are assigned to lambda terms; the exact nature of a type depends on the calculus considered (see <a href="/wiki/Typed_lambda_calculus#Kinds_of_typed_lambda_calculi" title="Typed lambda calculus">Kinds of typed lambda calculi</a>). From a certain point of view, typed lambda calculi can be seen as refinements of the untyped lambda calculus but from another point of view, they can also be considered the more fundamental theory and <i>untyped lambda calculus</i> a special case with only one type.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Typed lambda calculi are foundational programming languages and are the base of typed functional programming languages such as ML and Haskell and, more indirectly, typed <a href="/wiki/Imperative_programming" title="Imperative programming">imperative programming</a> languages. Typed lambda calculi play an important role in the design of <a href="/wiki/Type_system" title="Type system">type systems</a> for programming languages; here typability usually captures desirable properties of the program, e.g., the program will not cause a memory access violation.
</p><p>Typed lambda calculi are closely related to <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">mathematical logic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proof_theory" title="Proof theory">proof theory</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Curry%E2%80%93Howard_isomorphism" class="mw-redirect" title="Curry–Howard isomorphism">Curry–Howard isomorphism</a> and they can be considered as the <a href="/wiki/Internal_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Internal language">internal language</a> of classes of <a href="/wiki/Category_theory" title="Category theory">categories</a>, e.g., the simply typed lambda calculus is the language of a <a href="/wiki/Cartesian_closed_category" title="Cartesian closed category">Cartesian closed category</a> (CCC).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Reduction_strategies">Reduction strategies</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lambda_calculus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Reduction strategies"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Reduction_strategy#Lambda_calculus" title="Reduction strategy">Reduction strategy §&#160;Lambda calculus</a></div>
<p>Whether a term is normalising or not, and how much work needs to be done in normalising it if it is, depends to a large extent on the reduction strategy used. Common lambda calculus reduction strategies include:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Normal order</dt>
<dd>The leftmost outermost redex is reduced first. That is, whenever possible, arguments are substituted into the body of an abstraction before the arguments are reduced. If a term has a beta-normal form, normal order reduction will always reach that normal form.</dd>
<dt>Applicative order</dt>
<dd>The leftmost innermost redex is reduced first. As a consequence, a function's arguments are always reduced before they are substituted into the function. Unlike normal order reduction, applicative order reduction may fail to find the beta-normal form of an expression, even if such a normal form exists. For example, the term <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle (\;\lambda x.y\;\;(\lambda z.(zz)\;\lambda z.(zz))\;)}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mspace width="thickmathspace" />
        <mi>&#x03BB;<!-- λ --></mi>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo>.</mo>
        <mi>y</mi>
        <mspace width="thickmathspace" />
        <mspace width="thickmathspace" />
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>&#x03BB;<!-- λ --></mi>
        <mi>z</mi>
        <mo>.</mo>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>z</mi>
        <mi>z</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mspace width="thickmathspace" />
        <mi>&#x03BB;<!-- λ --></mi>
        <mi>z</mi>
        <mo>.</mo>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>z</mi>
        <mi>z</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mspace width="thickmathspace" />
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle (\;\lambda x.y\;\;(\lambda z.(zz)\;\lambda z.(zz))\;)}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2b4959d438e7ae2c61cf8732b962f9ead5718894" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:26.644ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle (\;\lambda x.y\;\;(\lambda z.(zz)\;\lambda z.(zz))\;)}"></span> is reduced to itself by applicative order, while normal order reduces it to its beta-normal form <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle y}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>y</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle y}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b8a6208ec717213d4317e666f1ae872e00620a0d" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.155ex; height:2.009ex;" alt="{\displaystyle y}"></span>.</dd>
<dt>Full β-reductions</dt>
<dd>Any redex can be reduced at any time. This means essentially the lack of any particular reduction strategy—with regard to reducibility, "all bets are off".</dd></dl>
<p>Weak reduction strategies do not reduce under lambda abstractions:
</p>
<dl><dt>Call by value<span class="anchor" id="callByValue"></span></dt>
<dd>Like applicative order, but no reductions are performed inside abstractions. This is similar to the evaluation order of strict languages like C: the arguments to a function are evaluated before calling the function, and function bodies are not even partially evaluated until the arguments are substituted in.</dd>
<dt>Call by name</dt>
<dd>Like normal order, but no reductions are performed inside abstractions. For example, <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">λ<i>x</i>.(λ<i>y</i>.<i>y</i>)<i>x</i></span> is in normal form according to this strategy, although it contains the redex <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">(λ<i>y</i>.<i>y</i>)<i>x</i></span>.</dd></dl>
<p>Strategies with sharing reduce computations that are "the same" in parallel:
</p>
<dl><dt>Optimal reduction</dt>
<dd>As normal order, but computations that have the same label are reduced simultaneously.</dd>
<dt>Call by need</dt>
<dd>As call by name (hence weak), but function applications that would duplicate terms instead name the argument. The argument may be evaluated "when needed", at which point the name binding is updated with the reduced value. This can save time compared to normal order evaluation.</dd></dl>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Computability">Computability</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lambda_calculus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Computability"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>There is no algorithm that takes as input any two lambda expressions and outputs <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">TRUE</span> or <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">FALSE</span> depending on whether one expression reduces to the other.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Church1936&#95;18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Church1936-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> More precisely, no <a href="/wiki/Computable_function" title="Computable function">computable function</a> can <a href="/wiki/Decision_problem" title="Decision problem">decide</a> the question. This was historically the first problem for which undecidability could be proven. As usual for such a proof, <i>computable</i> means computable by any <a href="/wiki/Model_of_computation" title="Model of computation">model of computation</a> that is <a href="/wiki/Turing_complete" class="mw-redirect" title="Turing complete">Turing complete</a>. In fact computability can itself be defined via the lambda calculus: a function <i>F</i>: <b>N</b> → <b>N</b> of natural numbers is a computable function if and only if there exists a lambda expression <i>f</i> such that for every pair of <i>x</i>, <i>y</i> in <b>N</b>, <i>F</i>(<i>x</i>)=<i>y</i> if and only if <i>f</i> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>x</i></span>&#160;=<sub>β</sub>&#160;<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>y</i></span>, where <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>x</i></span> and <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>y</i></span> are the <a href="/wiki/Church_numeral" class="mw-redirect" title="Church numeral">Church numerals</a> corresponding to <i>x</i> and <i>y</i>, respectively and =<sub>β</sub> meaning equivalence with β-reduction. See the <a href="/wiki/Church%E2%80%93Turing_thesis" title="Church–Turing thesis">Church–Turing thesis</a> for other approaches to defining computability and their equivalence.
</p><p>Church's proof of uncomputability first reduces the problem to determining whether a given lambda expression has a <a href="/wiki/Beta_normal_form" title="Beta normal form">normal form</a>. Then he assumes that this predicate is computable, and can hence be expressed in lambda calculus. Building on earlier work by Kleene and constructing a <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del_numbering" title="Gödel numbering">Gödel numbering</a> for lambda expressions, he constructs a lambda expression <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>e</i></span> that closely follows the proof of <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del%27s_incompleteness_theorems" title="Gödel&#39;s incompleteness theorems">Gödel's first incompleteness theorem</a>. If <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>e</i></span> is applied to its own Gödel number, a contradiction results.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Complexity">Complexity</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lambda_calculus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Complexity"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The notion of <a href="/wiki/Computational_complexity_theory" title="Computational complexity theory">computational complexity</a> for the lambda calculus is a bit tricky, because the cost of a β-reduction may vary depending on how it is implemented.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
To be precise, one must somehow find the location of all of the occurrences of the bound variable <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>V</i></span> in the expression <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>E</i></span>, implying a time cost, or one must keep track of the locations of free variables in some way, implying a space cost. A naïve search for the locations of <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>V</i></span> in <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>E</i></span> is <a href="/wiki/Big_O_notation" title="Big O notation"><i>O</i>(<i>n</i>)</a> in the length <i>n</i> of <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced"><i>E</i></span>. <a href="/wiki/Director_string" title="Director string">Director strings</a> were an early approach that traded this time cost for a quadratic space usage.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> More generally this has led to the study of systems that use <a href="/wiki/Explicit_substitution" title="Explicit substitution">explicit substitution</a>.
</p><p>In 2014, it was shown that the number of β-reduction steps taken by normal order reduction to reduce a term is a <i>reasonable</i> time cost model, that is, the reduction can be simulated on a Turing machine in time polynomially proportional to the number of steps.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This was a long-standing open problem, due to <i>size explosion</i>, the existence of lambda terms which grow exponentially in size for each β-reduction. The result gets around this by working with a compact shared representation. The result makes clear that the amount of space needed to evaluate a lambda term is not proportional to the size of the term during reduction. It is not currently known what a good measure of space complexity would be.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Reasonable&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reasonable-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>An unreasonable model does not necessarily mean inefficient. <a href="/wiki/Reduction_strategy#Optimal_reduction" title="Reduction strategy">Optimal reduction</a> reduces all computations with the same label in one step, avoiding duplicated work, but the number of parallel β-reduction steps to reduce a given term to normal form is approximately linear in the size of the term. This is far too small to be a reasonable cost measure, as any Turing machine may be encoded in the lambda calculus in size linearly proportional to the size of the Turing machine. The true cost of reducing lambda terms is not due to β-reduction per se but rather the handling of the duplication of redexes during β-reduction.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Asperti&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asperti-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is not known if optimal reduction implementations are reasonable when measured with respect to a reasonable cost model such as the number of leftmost-outermost steps to normal form, but it has been shown for fragments of the lambda calculus that the optimal reduction algorithm is efficient and has at most a quadratic overhead compared to leftmost-outermost.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Reasonable&#95;42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reasonable-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In addition the BOHM prototype implementation of optimal reduction outperformed both <a href="/wiki/Caml" title="Caml">Caml</a> Light and Haskell on pure lambda terms.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Asperti&#95;43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asperti-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Lambda_calculus_and_programming_languages">Lambda calculus and programming languages</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lambda_calculus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Lambda calculus and programming languages"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>As pointed out by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Landin" title="Peter Landin">Peter Landin</a>'s 1965 paper "<i>A Correspondence between <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60" title="ALGOL 60">ALGOL 60</a> and Church's Lambda-notation</i>",<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> sequential <a href="/wiki/Procedural_programming" title="Procedural programming">procedural programming</a> languages can be understood in terms of the lambda calculus, which provides the basic mechanisms for procedural abstraction and procedure (subprogram) application.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Anonymous_functions">Anonymous functions</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lambda_calculus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Anonymous functions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Anonymous_function" title="Anonymous function">Anonymous function</a></div>
<p>For example, in <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a> the "square" function can be expressed as a lambda expression as follows:
</p>
<div class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-python mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr"><pre><span></span><span class="p">(</span><span class="k">lambda</span> <span class="n">x</span><span class="p">:</span> <span class="n">x</span><span class="o">**</span><span class="mi">2</span><span class="p">)</span>
</pre></div>
<p>The above example is an expression that evaluates to a first-class function. The symbol <code>lambda</code> creates an anonymous function, given a list of parameter names—just the single argument <code>x</code>, in this case—and an expression that is evaluated as the body of the function, <code>x**2</code>. Anonymous functions are sometimes called <i>lambda expressions.</i>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pascal_(programming_language)" title="Pascal (programming language)">Pascal</a> and many other imperative languages have long supported passing <a href="/wiki/Function_(computer_programming)" title="Function (computer programming)">subprograms</a> as <a href="/wiki/Parameter_(computer_programming)" title="Parameter (computer programming)">arguments</a> to other subprograms through the mechanism of <a href="/wiki/Function_pointer" title="Function pointer">function pointers</a>. However, function pointers are an insufficient condition for functions to be <a href="/wiki/First-class_function" title="First-class function">first class</a> datatypes, because a function is a first class datatype if and only if new instances of the function can be created at <a href="/wiki/Execution_(computing)#runtime" title="Execution (computing)">runtime</a>. Such runtime creation of functions is supported in <a href="/wiki/Smalltalk" title="Smalltalk">Smalltalk</a>, <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wolfram_Language" title="Wolfram Language">Wolfram Language</a>, and more recently in <a href="/wiki/Scala_(programming_language)" title="Scala (programming language)">Scala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eiffel_(programming_language)" title="Eiffel (programming language)">Eiffel</a> (as agents), <a href="/wiki/C_Sharp_(programming_language)" title="C Sharp (programming language)">C#</a> (as delegates) and <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B11" title="C++11">C++11</a>, among others.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Parallelism_and_concurrency">Parallelism and concurrency</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lambda_calculus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Parallelism and concurrency"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Church%E2%80%93Rosser_theorem" title="Church–Rosser theorem">Church–Rosser</a> property of the lambda calculus means that evaluation (β-reduction) can be carried out in <i>any order</i>, even in parallel. This means that various <a href="/wiki/Nondeterministic_algorithm" title="Nondeterministic algorithm">nondeterministic</a> <a href="/wiki/Evaluation_strategy" title="Evaluation strategy">evaluation strategies</a> are relevant. However, the lambda calculus does not offer any explicit constructs for <a href="/wiki/Parallel_computing" title="Parallel computing">parallelism</a>. One can add constructs such as <a href="/wiki/Futures_and_promises" title="Futures and promises">futures</a> to the lambda calculus. Other <a href="/wiki/Process_calculi" class="mw-redirect" title="Process calculi">process calculi</a> have been developed for describing communication and concurrency.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Semantics">Semantics</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lambda_calculus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Semantics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The fact that lambda calculus terms act as functions on other lambda calculus terms, and even on themselves, led to questions about the semantics of the lambda calculus. Could a sensible meaning be assigned to lambda calculus terms? The natural semantics was to find a set <i>D</i> isomorphic to the function space <i>D</i> → <i>D</i>, of functions on itself. However, no nontrivial such <i>D</i> can exist, by <a href="/wiki/Cardinality" title="Cardinality">cardinality</a> constraints because the set of all functions from <i>D</i> to <i>D</i> has greater cardinality than <i>D</i>, unless <i>D</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Singleton_set" class="mw-redirect" title="Singleton set">singleton set</a>.
</p><p>In the 1970s, <a href="/wiki/Dana_Scott" title="Dana Scott">Dana Scott</a> showed that if only <a href="/wiki/Scott_continuity" title="Scott continuity">continuous functions</a> were considered, a set or <a href="/wiki/Domain_theory" title="Domain theory">domain</a> <i>D</i> with the required property could be found, thus providing a <a href="/wiki/Model_theory" title="Model theory">model</a> for the lambda calculus.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>This work also formed the basis for the <a href="/wiki/Denotational_semantics" title="Denotational semantics">denotational semantics</a> of programming languages.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Variations_and_extensions">Variations and extensions</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lambda_calculus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Variations and extensions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>These extensions are in the <a href="/wiki/Lambda_cube" title="Lambda cube">lambda cube</a>:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Typed_lambda_calculus" title="Typed lambda calculus">Typed lambda calculus</a> – Lambda calculus with typed variables (and functions)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/System_F" title="System F">System F</a> – A typed lambda calculus with type-variables</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Calculus_of_constructions" title="Calculus of constructions">Calculus of constructions</a> – A typed lambda calculus with types as first-class values</li></ul>
<p>These <a href="/wiki/Formal_system" title="Formal system">formal systems</a> are extensions of lambda calculus that are not in the lambda cube:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Binary_lambda_calculus" class="mw-redirect" title="Binary lambda calculus">Binary lambda calculus</a> – A version of lambda calculus with binary <a href="/wiki/Input/output" title="Input/output">input/output</a> (I/O), a binary encoding of terms, and a designated universal machine.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lambda-mu_calculus" title="Lambda-mu calculus">Lambda-mu calculus</a> – An extension of the lambda calculus for treating <a href="/wiki/Classical_logic" title="Classical logic">classical logic</a></li></ul>
<p>These formal systems are variations of lambda calculus:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Kappa_calculus" title="Kappa calculus">Kappa calculus</a> – A first-order analogue of lambda calculus</li></ul>
<p>These formal systems are related to lambda calculus:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Combinatory_logic" title="Combinatory logic">Combinatory logic</a> – A notation for mathematical logic without variables</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/SKI_combinator_calculus" title="SKI combinator calculus">SKI combinator calculus</a> – A computational system based on the <b><a href="#S">S</a></b>, <b><a href="#K">K</a></b> and <b><a href="#I">I</a></b> combinators, equivalent to lambda calculus, but reducible without variable substitutions</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="See_also">See also</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lambda_calculus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: See also"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1184024115">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 30em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Applicative_computing_systems" title="Applicative computing systems">Applicative computing systems</a> – Treatment of <a href="/wiki/Object_(computer_science)" title="Object (computer science)">objects</a> in the style of the lambda calculus</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cartesian_closed_category" title="Cartesian closed category">Cartesian closed category</a> – A setting for lambda calculus in <a href="/wiki/Category_theory" title="Category theory">category theory</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Categorical_abstract_machine" title="Categorical abstract machine">Categorical abstract machine</a> – A <a href="/wiki/Model_of_computation" title="Model of computation">model of computation</a> applicable to lambda calculus</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Clojure" title="Clojure">Clojure</a>, programming language</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Curry%E2%80%93Howard_isomorphism" class="mw-redirect" title="Curry–Howard isomorphism">Curry–Howard isomorphism</a> – The formal correspondence between programs and <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_proof" title="Mathematical proof">proofs</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/De_Bruijn_index" title="De Bruijn index">De Bruijn index</a> – notation disambiguating alpha conversions</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/De_Bruijn_notation" title="De Bruijn notation">De Bruijn notation</a> – notation using postfix modification functions</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Domain_theory" title="Domain theory">Domain theory</a> – Study of certain <a href="/wiki/Partially_ordered_sets" class="mw-redirect" title="Partially ordered sets">posets</a> giving <a href="/wiki/Denotational_semantics" title="Denotational semantics">denotational semantics</a> for lambda calculus</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Evaluation_strategy" title="Evaluation strategy">Evaluation strategy</a> – Rules for the evaluation of expressions in <a href="/wiki/Programming_language" title="Programming language">programming languages</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Explicit_substitution" title="Explicit substitution">Explicit substitution</a> – The theory of substitution, as used in <a href="#β-reduction">β-reduction</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harrop_formula" title="Harrop formula">Harrop formula</a> – A kind of constructive logical formula such that proofs are lambda terms</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Interaction_nets" title="Interaction nets">Interaction nets</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kleene%E2%80%93Rosser_paradox" title="Kleene–Rosser paradox">Kleene–Rosser paradox</a> – A demonstration that some form of lambda calculus is inconsistent</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Knights_of_the_Lambda_Calculus" title="Knights of the Lambda Calculus">Knights of the Lambda Calculus</a> – A semi-fictional organization of LISP and <a href="/wiki/Scheme_(programming_language)" title="Scheme (programming language)">Scheme</a> <a href="/wiki/Hacker_(programmer_subculture)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hacker (programmer subculture)">hackers</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Krivine_machine" title="Krivine machine">Krivine machine</a> – An abstract machine to interpret call-by-name in lambda calculus</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lambda_calculus_definition" title="Lambda calculus definition">Lambda calculus definition</a> – Formal definition of the lambda calculus.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Let_expression" title="Let expression">Let expression</a> – An expression closely related to an abstraction.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Minimalism_(computing)" title="Minimalism (computing)">Minimalism (computing)</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rewriting" title="Rewriting">Rewriting</a> – Transformation of formulæ in formal systems</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/SECD_machine" title="SECD machine">SECD machine</a> – A <a href="/wiki/Virtual_machine" title="Virtual machine">virtual machine</a> designed for the lambda calculus</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Scott%E2%80%93Curry_theorem" title="Scott–Curry theorem">Scott–Curry theorem</a> – A theorem about sets of lambda terms</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/To_Mock_a_Mockingbird" title="To Mock a Mockingbird">To Mock a Mockingbird</a></i> – An introduction to <a href="/wiki/Combinatory_logic" title="Combinatory logic">combinatory logic</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Universal_Turing_machine" title="Universal Turing machine">Universal Turing machine</a> – A formal computing machine equivalent to lambda calculus</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Unlambda" title="Unlambda">Unlambda</a> – A functional <a href="/wiki/Esoteric_programming_language" title="Esoteric programming language">esoteric programming language</a> based on combinatory logic</li></ul>
</div>

<li>Church, Alonzo, <i>An unsolvable problem of elementary number theory</i>, <a href="/wiki/American_Journal_of_Mathematics" title="American Journal of Mathematics">American Journal of Mathematics</a>, 58 (1936), pp.&#160;345–363. This paper contains the proof that the equivalence of lambda expressions is in general not decidable.</li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFChurch1941" class="citation book cs1">Church, Alonzo (1941). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/AnnalsOfMathematicalStudies6ChurchAlonzoTheCalculiOfLambdaConversionPrincetonUniversityPress1941"><i>The Calculi of Lambda-Conversion</i></a>. Princeton: Princeton University Press<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2020-04-14</span></span>.</cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Calculi+of+Lambda-Conversion&amp;rft.place=Princeton&amp;rft.pub=Princeton+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1941&amp;rft.aulast=Church&amp;rft.aufirst=Alonzo&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2FAnnalsOfMathematicalStudies6ChurchAlonzoTheCalculiOfLambdaConversionPrincetonUniversityPress1941&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALambda+calculus" class="Z3988"></span> (<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-691-08394-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-691-08394-0"><bdi>978-0-691-08394-0</bdi></a>)</li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFFrink&#95;Jr.,&#95;Orrin1944" class="citation journal cs1"><a href="/wiki/Orrin_Frink" title="Orrin Frink">Frink Jr., Orrin</a> (1944). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.ams.org/bull/1944-50-03/S0002-9904-1944-08090-7/S0002-9904-1944-08090-7.pdf">"Review: <i>The Calculi of Lambda-Conversion</i> by Alonzo Church"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>Bulletin of the American Mathematical Society</i>. <b>50</b> (3): <span class="nowrap">169–</span>172. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="id-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1090%2Fs0002-9904-1944-08090-7">10.1090/s0002-9904-1944-08090-7</a></span>.</cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Bulletin+of+the+American+Mathematical+Society&amp;rft.atitle=Review%3A+The+Calculi+of+Lambda-Conversion+by+Alonzo+Church&amp;rft.volume=50&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=169-172&amp;rft.date=1944&amp;rft&#95;id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1090%2Fs0002-9904-1944-08090-7&amp;rft.au=Frink+Jr.%2C+Orrin&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.ams.org%2Fbull%2F1944-50-03%2FS0002-9904-1944-08090-7%2FS0002-9904-1944-08090-7.pdf&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALambda+calculus" class="Z3988"></span></li>
<li>Kleene, Stephen, <i>A theory of positive integers in formal logic</i>, <a href="/wiki/American_Journal_of_Mathematics" title="American Journal of Mathematics">American Journal of Mathematics</a>, 57 (1935), pp.&#160;153–173 and 219–244. Contains the lambda calculus definitions of several familiar functions.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Peter_Landin" title="Peter Landin">Landin, Peter</a>, <i>A Correspondence Between ALGOL 60 and Church's Lambda-Notation</i>, <a href="/wiki/Communications_of_the_ACM" title="Communications of the ACM">Communications of the ACM</a>, vol. 8, no. 2 (1965), pages 89–101. Available from the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://portal.acm.org/citation.cfm?id=363749&amp;coll=portal&amp;dl=ACM">ACM site</a>. A classic paper highlighting the importance of lambda calculus as a basis for programming languages.</li>
<li>Larson, Jim, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20011206080336/http://www.jetcafe.org/~jim/lambda.html"><i>An Introduction to Lambda Calculus and Scheme</i></a>. A gentle introduction for programmers.</li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFMichaelson2013" class="citation book cs1">Michaelson, Greg (10 April 2013). <i>An Introduction to Functional Programming Through Lambda Calculus</i>. Courier Corporation. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-486-28029-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-486-28029-5"><bdi>978-0-486-28029-5</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=An+Introduction+to+Functional+Programming+Through+Lambda+Calculus&amp;rft.pub=Courier+Corporation&amp;rft.date=2013-04-10&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-486-28029-5&amp;rft.aulast=Michaelson&amp;rft.aufirst=Greg&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALambda+calculus" class="Z3988"></span><sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Schalk, A. and Simmons, H. (2005) <i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080307014129/http://www.cs.man.ac.uk/~hsimmons/BOOKS/lcalculus.pdf">An introduction to λ-calculi and arithmetic with a decent selection of exercises</a></i>. Notes for a course in the Mathematical Logic MSc at Manchester University.</li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFde&#95;Queiroz2008" class="citation journal cs1"><a href="/wiki/Ruy_de_Queiroz" title="Ruy de Queiroz">de Queiroz, Ruy J.G.B.</a> (2008). "On Reduction Rules, Meaning-as-Use and Proof-Theoretic Semantics". <i><a href="/wiki/Studia_Logica" title="Studia Logica">Studia Logica</a></i>. <b>90</b> (2): <span class="nowrap">211–</span>247. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1007%2Fs11225-008-9150-5">10.1007/s11225-008-9150-5</a>. <a href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:11321602">11321602</a>.</cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Studia+Logica&amp;rft.atitle=On+Reduction+Rules%2C+Meaning-as-Use+and+Proof-Theoretic+Semantics&amp;rft.volume=90&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.pages=211-247&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft&#95;id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1007%2Fs11225-008-9150-5&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A11321602%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft.aulast=de+Queiroz&amp;rft.aufirst=Ruy+J.G.B.&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALambda+calculus" class="Z3988"></span> A paper giving a formal underpinning to the idea of 'meaning-is-use' which, even if based on proofs, it is different from proof-theoretic semantics as in the Dummett–Prawitz tradition since it takes reduction as the rules giving meaning.</li>
<li>Hankin, Chris, <i>An Introduction to Lambda Calculi for Computer Scientists,</i> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0954300653" title="Special:BookSources/0954300653"><bdi>0954300653</bdi></a></li></ul>
<dl><dt>Monographs/textbooks for graduate students</dt>
<dd></dd></dl>
<ul><li>Sørensen, Morten Heine and Urzyczyn, Paweł (2006), <i>Lectures on the Curry–Howard isomorphism</i>, Elsevier, <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-444-52077-5" title="Special:BookSources/0-444-52077-5"><bdi>0-444-52077-5</bdi></a> is a recent monograph that covers the main topics of lambda calculus from the type-free variety, to most <a href="/wiki/Typed_lambda_calculi" class="mw-redirect" title="Typed lambda calculi">typed lambda calculi</a>, including more recent developments like <a href="/wiki/Pure_type_system" title="Pure type system">pure type systems</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lambda_cube" title="Lambda cube">lambda cube</a>. It does not cover <a href="/wiki/Subtyping" title="Subtyping">subtyping</a> extensions.</li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFPierce2002" class="citation cs2">Pierce, Benjamin (2002), <i>Types and Programming Languages</i>, MIT Press, <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-262-16209-1" title="Special:BookSources/0-262-16209-1"><bdi>0-262-16209-1</bdi></a></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Types+and+Programming+Languages&amp;rft.pub=MIT+Press&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.isbn=0-262-16209-1&amp;rft.aulast=Pierce&amp;rft.aufirst=Benjamin&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALambda+calculus" class="Z3988"></span> covers lambda calculi from a practical type system perspective; some topics like dependent types are only mentioned, but subtyping is an important topic.</li></ul>
<dl><dt>Documents</dt></dl>
<ul><li><i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.cs.bham.ac.uk/~axj/pub/papers/lambda-calculus.pdf">A Short Introduction to the Lambda Calculus</a></i>-(<a href="/wiki/Portable_Document_Format" class="mw-redirect" title="Portable Document Format">PDF</a>) by Achim Jung</li>
<li><i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://turing100.acm.org/lambda_calculus_timeline.pdf">A timeline of lambda calculus</a></i>-(<a href="/wiki/Portable_Document_Format" class="mw-redirect" title="Portable Document Format">PDF</a>) by Dana Scott</li>
<li><i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.inf.fu-berlin.de/inst/ag-ki/rojas_home/documents/tutorials/lambda.pdf">A Tutorial Introduction to the Lambda Calculus</a></i>-(<a href="/wiki/Portable_Document_Format" class="mw-redirect" title="Portable Document Format">PDF</a>) by Raúl Rojas</li>
<li><i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mscs.dal.ca/~selinger/papers/#lambdanotes">Lecture Notes on the Lambda Calculus</a></i>-(<a href="/wiki/Portable_Document_Format" class="mw-redirect" title="Portable Document Format">PDF</a>) by Peter Selinger</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140202195546/http://imar.ro/~mbuliga/graphic_revised.pdf"><i>Graphic lambda calculus</i></a> by Marius Buliga</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160729210437/http://cs.adelaide.edu.au/~pmk/publications/wage2008.pdf"><i>Lambda Calculus as a Workflow Model</i></a> by Peter Kelly, Paul Coddington, and Andrew Wendelborn; mentions <a href="/wiki/Graph_reduction" title="Graph reduction">graph reduction</a> as a common means of evaluating lambda expressions and discusses the applicability of lambda calculus for <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a> (due to the <a href="/wiki/Church%E2%80%93Rosser_theorem" title="Church–Rosser theorem">Church–Rosser</a> property, which enables <a href="/wiki/Parallel_computing" title="Parallel computing">parallel</a> graph reduction for lambda expressions).</li></ul>

<li id="cite&#95;note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"where M[x&#160;:= N] denotes the substitution of N for every occurrence of x in M".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-BarendregtBarendsen&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BarendregtBarendsen-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7">&#58;&#8202;7&#8202;</span></sup> Also denoted M[N/x], "the substitution of N for x in M".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-beta-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-beta_5-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Barendregt, Barendsen (2000) call this rule <i>axiom β</i></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For a full history, see Cardone and Hindley's "History of Lambda-calculus and Combinatory Logic" (2006).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-mapsTo-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-mapsTo_23-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-mapsTo_23-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle \mapsto }">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mo stretchy="false">&#x21A6;<!-- ↦ --></mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mapsto }</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bc09de045e7d82eef9fe078e7e7606576640c11b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.324ex; height:1.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle \mapsto }"></span> is pronounced "<a href="/wiki/Maplet" class="mw-redirect" title="Maplet">maps to</a>".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">(λ<i>f</i>.</span><i>M</i><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">)</span> <i>N</i> can be pronounced "let f be N in M".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-ariola-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ariola_29-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ariola and Blom<sup id="cite&#95;ref-AB94&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AB94-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> employ 1) axioms for a representational calculus using <i>well-formed cyclic lambda graphs</i> extended with <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" /><span class="monospaced">letrec</span>, to detect possibly infinite unwinding trees; 2) the representational calculus with β-reduction of scoped lambda graphs constitute Ariola/Blom's cyclic extension of lambda calculus; 3) Ariola/Blom reason about strict languages using <a href="#callByValue">§ call-by-value</a>, and compare to Moggi's calculus, and to Hasegawa's calculus. Conclusions on p. 111.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-AB94&#95;28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AB94-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>

<li><i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.safalra.com/science/lambda-calculus/">Lambda Calculus</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20121014180848/http://safalra.com/science/lambda-calculus/">Archived</a> 2012-10-14 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></i> on <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.safalra.com/">Safalra's Website</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210502051230/https://safalra.com/">Archived</a> 2021-05-02 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://chatziko.github.io/lci/">LCI Lambda Interpreter</a> a simple yet powerful pure calculus interpreter</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://lambda-the-ultimate.org/classic/lc.html">Lambda Calculus links on Lambda-the-Ultimate</a></li>

<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://matt.might.net/articles/c++-template-meta-programming-with-lambda-calculus/">Implementing the Lambda calculus</a> using <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B_Templates" class="mw-redirect" title="C++ Templates">C++ Templates</a></li>
